If you're confused, they're making ICE CREAM mochi, which is ice cream wrapped in mochi skin. Regular traditional Japanese mochi DOES NOT have ice cream in it. Usually mochi has red bean paste and a strawberry in it (daifuku), but then we can be real complicated and get into kirimochi, which is like a boiled rice cake that can be flavored it a lot of ways (mostly savory).

Sticky rice is sticky because it has amylopectin. Which is why it is called glutenous rice (it does NOT have gluten, rice doesnâ€™t contain gluten). The amylopectin reacts with hot water and creates a melting type effect (think of solder welding) and causes the rice to stick to each other. #ThankYouFoodScienceDegree

You have different types of rice, long grain and short/small grain. The long grains are dryer like basmati and white rice and the smaller grains are stickier like jasmin rice and sushi rice. The small grains are stickier because of the amount of starch they  contain! You also have broken grains which are leftovers from production and they can be used as dessert rice. Because they're broken they can absorb lots of moisture!

It's mochi fact time!  Mochi is a Japanese sticky rice paste. Mochi with ice cream inside isn't just called "mochi" it's called Mochi Daifuku. It is popular in Japan to wrap mochi around many things like ice cream, anko (sweet red bean paste), strawberries, ect. Anything that consists of mochi wrapped around something sweet is called mochi daifuku. Mochi is traditionally made by steaming mochi rice (a type of sweet rice) and then pounding it into a paste with a mallet in a large wooden cauldron. It requires 2 people, one to swing the heavy mallet, and the other to knead the mochi and put water on it between swings of the hammer. This takes intense focus and coordination with the other person so you don't crush someone's hand! Some people are so good at this and can go so fast it's insane, look up high speed mochi making videos. You won't be disappointed! What makes mochi sticky is the gluten in the rice.  Mochi is most commonly used in deserts such as mochi daifuku. It can be eaten on it's own, or with toppings on it such as sesame seeds or kinako (toasted soybean flour). But mochi is also loved in savory dishes. It can be put in soups, or grilled and dipped in soy sauce or wrapped in nori seaweed.  Mochi flour is called Mochiko. It's a simply easy way to make mochi, but it doesn't taste the same as fresh rice pounded mochi.  Fun Fact! In Japan, the term "mochi mochi" is used to describe something that has a pleasantly squishy texture. Many young girls want to have "mochi mochi" skin.  Hope you all enjoyed the mochi facts!

Mochi is actually just a rice cake made from mochigome, a Japanese short grain rice. It was adapted to a dessert when people thought to wrap it around ice cream. I used to just have mochi as a topping. Mochi ice cream is fairly new (from the early 90s I believe) and only recently became popular in the US. Just wanted to tell you if there's ice cream, it's called mochi ice cream. Not just mochi (that's the rice cake by itself) ğŸ˜

I was hoping you were making real Mochi ( I am Japanese and every year in December we make it.
